Let's analyze the recurrence more systematically.

We have a sequence a_n with a_{n+1}=s(a_n). We want all a_1 such that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. Since the sequence is infinite, it must not hit a prime or a square of a prime. But could it hit a number with exactly three proper divisors that is not a prime? That would be p^3, which has exactly three proper divisors. That's allowed. So we need to avoid primes and p^2. Also note that 1 has no proper divisors, so not allowed. So the allowed set is all integers with at least three proper divisors.

We need to determine the set of initial values that avoid primes and p^2 forever.

One approach: Show that for any m that is not a multiple of 6, s(m) is less than m? Not exactly. For m=12 (multiple of 6) s(12)=13>12. For m=30 (multiple of 6) s(30)=31>30. Perhaps we can prove that if a_1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence either strictly increases until it reaches a fixed point or strictly decreases until it reaches a prime? But we saw 72 increased to 78 (fixed), but 72 is not a fixed point. So it increased. Could there be numbers that increase beyond fixed points? Thus we need to analyze the function s(m). Perhaps we can characterize the possible values of s(m) in terms of the prime factorization. Let m be composite with at least three proper divisors. Actually as before, the divisors in decreasing order correspond to m divided by the divisors in increasing order. So if we list the divisors of m in increasing order: 1 = d_1 < d_2 < d_3 < ... < d_k = m. Then the proper divisors in decreasing order are m/d_2, m/d_3, ..., m/d_{k-1}. If m is a prime power p^a, then the divisors are 1, p, p^2, ..., p^a. So d_2 = p, d_3 = p^2, d_4 = p^3, etc. So s(m) = m/p + m/p^2 + m/p^3 = m/p^3 (p^2+p+1). For m = p^a, s(m) = p^{a-3}(p^2+p+1). For a≥3, s(m) < m if p^2+p+1 < p^3? Actually p^3 - (p^2+p+1) = p^3-p^2-p-1 = (p^3-p^2) - (p+1) = p^2(p-1) - (p+1). For p=2: 4*1-3=1 >0, so 2^3=8, s(8)=4+2+1=7<8. We need to analyze the function s more deeply.

Let’s define the set of divisors of n in increasing order: 1, p, p_2, p_3, ... where p is the smallest prime factor. Then the divisors of m are all numbers 2^j * d_i. To find the three largest proper divisors, we need the three smallest divisors >1 (d2,d3,d4). Since d2=2 always. Then d3 = min(4, p) if p exists? Actually we need to compare 4 and p. Since p is an odd prime, p ≥ 3. So if k≥2, then 4 is a divisor. So d3 = min(4, p). But if k=1, then 4 is not a divisor, so d3 = p (the smallest odd divisor >1). Similarly, d4 is the next smallest after d3.

Now, we also have the case where n=1, then there is no odd divisor >1, so the divisors are 2,4,8,... So d3=4, d4=8.

Now, the three largest proper divisors are: m/d2 = m/2 = k, then m/d3, then m/d4. So f(m) = m/2 + m/d3 + m/d4.

We want to know when iterating f eventually yields a fixed point.

We can think of the sequence as a dynamical system on the set of integers. Perhaps we can prove that the only numbers that can have an infinite orbit are those that are multiples of 6 and also have the property that the exponent of 2 is 1? But we saw 72 works, and its exponent of 2 is 3. So that's not true. However, 72 leads to 78, which is a fixed point with exponent 1. So maybe the orbit eventually becomes a fixed point with exponent 1. So we need to characterize the preimages of fixed points under f.

We can try to find all m such that f(m) is a fixed point. That is, f(m) = m' where m' is a fixed point. Then starting from m, we get to fixed point. So we need to find all m for which f(m) is in the fixed point set S (which is multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5). But also we need that the intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors. So we need to ensure that f(m) itself has at least three proper divisors, which is true if it is a fixed point (since fixed points have many proper divisors). Also we need that m itself has at least three proper divisors, which is given. So if we can show that for any m not a fixed point, either f(m) is a fixed point (then m works) or f(m) leads to a prime or p^2 eventually, then the set of possible a_1 is exactly the set of numbers whose orbit eventually lands on a fixed point. That set might be all numbers that are multiples of 6? But we saw 12,30,36,48,60,84,90,96,108,120,132,144,150,156,168,180,192,210,216,228,240,252,264,270,276,288,300? Let's test 300: 300=2^2*3*5^2, divisors:1,2,3,4,5,6,10,12,15,20,25,30,50,60,75,100,150,300. f(300)=150+100+75=325; 325=5^2*13, proper:13,5,1? Actually 325 divisors:1,5,13,25,65,325. So f(325)=65+13+5=83 prime -> fails. So 300 fails. So not all multiples of 6.

So we need to find a characterization.

Observation: Fixed points are numbers m such that the three largest proper divisors sum to m. This is equivalent to m being a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. So S = { m : 6|m, 4∤m, 5∤m }. Note that this set includes numbers that are multiples of 6 but also have exponent of 2 exactly 1. For m in S, we have m=6k with k odd and 5∤k. So S is infinite.

Now, we want to find all m such that iterating f eventually reaches S. Since f is deterministic, we can think of the directed graph on integers. We need to find all nodes that can reach S without hitting a sink (primes or p^2). Possibly the only sinks are primes and p^2, and all numbers that are not in S eventually either go to a sink or to S. So the set of possible a_1 is exactly the set of numbers that are not eventually mapped to a sink, i.e., the basin of attraction of S under f, provided that the iteration does not go through a number with fewer than three proper divisors (which would be a sink). But note: If a number maps to a prime or p^2, that's a dead end. So the only way to have infinite sequence is to never map to a prime or p^2. So the orbit must stay in the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all m such that the orbit under f remains in that set forever. Since the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors is infinite, but the dynamics might be such that most numbers eventually go to a prime or p^2. So we need to characterize those that avoid that.

Perhaps we can prove that if m is not a multiple of 6, then f(m) < m and eventually becomes prime. So the only possible a_1 that can avoid failure are multiples of 6. But we already saw that 12 is multiple of 6 but fails. So we need a stronger condition: Among multiples of 6, only those that are not divisible by 5? But 30 is multiple of 6 and divisible by 5, fails. 60 fails, 90 fails, 120 fails, 150 fails, 180 fails, 210 fails, 240 fails, 270 fails, 300 fails. So it seems that multiples of 6 that are divisible by 5 fail. But also some multiples of 6 not divisible by 5 fail: 12, 36, 48, 84, 96, 108, 132, 144, 168, 192, 216, 228, 252, 264, 276, 288, etc. So it's not just divisibility by 5. So what is the condition? Let's list the multiples of 6 up to maybe 200 and mark which ones lead to a fixed point or fail.

We'll compute f(m) for m multiples of 6 and see if the orbit reaches S. We can compute iteratively.

First, we need to know the fixed points S: m=6k with k odd and 5∤k. So k ∈ {1,3,7,9,11,13,17,19,21,23,27,29,31,33,37,39,41,43,47,49,51,53,57,59,61,63,67,69,71,73,77,79,81,83,87,89,91,93,97,99,101,103,107,109,113,117,119,121,123,129,131,133,137,139,141,143,147,149,151,153,157,159,161,163,167,169,171,173,177,179,181,183,187,189,191,193,197,199,...} but note that k must be odd and not divisible by 5, but also we need to ensure that m has at least three proper divisors? For m=6, k=1 works; m=18, k=3 works; m=42, k=7 works; m=54, k=9 works; m=66, k=11 works; m=78, k=13 works; m=102, k=17 works; m=114, k=19 works; m=126, k=21 works; m=138, k=23 works; m=162, k=27 works; m=174, k=29 works; m=186, k=31 works; m=198, k=33 works; m=222, k=37 works; m=234, k=39 works; m=258? 258=6*43, k=43 works; etc. So all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5 are fixed points.

Now, for other multiples of 6, we need to compute f(m). Let's compute f(m) for m=12,18,24,30,36,42,48,54,60,66,72,78,84,90,96,102,108,114,120,126,132,138,144,150,156,162,168,174,180,186,192,198,204,210,216,222,228,234,240,246,252,258,264,270,276,282,288,294,300,306,312,318,324,330,336,342,348,354,360,366,372,378,384,390,396,402,408,414,420,426,432,438,444,450,456,462,468,474,480,486,492,498,504,510,516,522,528,534,540,546,552,558,564,570,576,582,588,594,600,606,612,618,624,630,636,642,648,654,660,666,672,678,684,690,696,702,708,714,720,726,732,738,744,750,756,762,768,774,780,786,792,798,804,810,816,822,828,834,840,846,852,858,864,870,876,882,888,894,900,906,912,918,924,930,936,942,948,954,960,966,972,978,984,990,996,1002, etc.

We see that many multiples of 6 that are not divisible by 5 and have exponent of 2 exactly 1 (i.e., k odd and not divisible by 5) are fixed points. But also some numbers with exponent of 2>1 and not divisible by 5 like 72, 216, 648, 792, 882, 918, 966, 1002, etc., work because they map to a fixed point. So the condition seems to be: a_1 is a multiple of 6, and also a_1 is not divisible by 5? But 12 is divisible by 6 and not by 5, yet fails. So why does 12 fail? 12 maps to 13 prime. So maybe the condition is that a_1 is a multiple of 6 and also a_1 is not divisible by 5, and also a_1 is not equal to 12? But 12 is just one case. Let's try to analyze the function s(m) in terms of the prime factorization more systematically.

But the condition that each term has at least three proper divisors is a restriction on the orbit. Since the problem asks for all possible a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors), we need to find all a_1 for which the orbit never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. - Lemma: If N is a positive integer with at least three proper divisors, then define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. Then f(N) is at most N? Actually not, but we can analyze.

We need to prove that the sequence exists for all n if and only if a_1 is in the described set.

Proof approach:

Now, we also need to consider numbers that are not fixed but lead to fixed points. We'll show that if N is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, and divisible by 4, then either N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 odd, in which case f(N) is a fixed point (and then the sequence becomes constant), or N is not a multiple of 72, in which case the sequence eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors (i.e., a prime or p^2). Also, we need to show that if 6∤N, then the sequence fails.

Let's analyze the general behavior.

And that for any other m, the orbit leads to a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

So the pattern is clear: The only numbers that work are those that are multiples of 6, not divisible by 5, and either not divisible by 4 (a=1) or divisible by 72 with the quotient odd and not divisible by 5. So the set is: all N such that 6|N, 5∤N, and (4∤N or 72|N and N/72 is odd). Since if 4∤N, then automatically 72∤N, so the condition is simply: N is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, and if N is divisible by 4 then N must be a multiple of 72 and N/72 must be odd.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. We can structure the proof as follows:

- Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.
- Lemma 1: f(N)=N if and only if N=6k with k odd and 5∤k. (Prove by analyzing the three smallest divisors >1.)
- Lemma 2: For any N with at least three proper divisors, if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N. Moreover, the sequence is strictly decreasing and eventually reaches a prime or a square of a prime (which have fewer than three proper divisors). So such a_1 cannot yield an infinite sequence.
- Lemma 3: If N is a multiple of 6 and 5|N, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6, so the sequence will strictly decrease from f(N) onward and eventually hit a prime or square. So such a_1 fails.
- Lemma 4: If N is a multiple of 6 and 5∤N, then we consider the exponent of 2 in N. Let N=2^a * 3 * t, where t is coprime to 6. Then:
   - If a=1, then f(N)=N (by Lemma 1). So the sequence is constant.

Now, we can check that numbers like 72,216,504,648,792,936, etc., satisfy this. Also numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,222, etc., satisfy because 4∤N.

Thus, the answer is: All positive integers N such that 6 divides N, 5 does not divide N, and either 4 does not divide N or 72 divides N and N/72 is odd.

We can also write this as: N = 6k where k is odd and 5∤k, or N = 72t where t is odd and 5∤t. Thus, the characterization is:

- If N is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a prime (and fails).
- If N is a multiple of 6, then:
   * If 4 ∤ N and 5 ∤ N, then N is a fixed point (sequence constant).
   * If 4 | N and 5 ∤ N, then let N = 12k. Then f(N) = 13k. For the sequence to not fail, we need that f(N) is a fixed point. This requires that k is a multiple of 6? Actually f(N) = 13k. For f(N) to be a multiple of 6, we need k to be a multiple of 6 (since 13 odd, so need 2|k, and also 3|k). So k must be a multiple of 6. Then N = 12k = 12*6s = 72s. Then f(N) = 13*6s = 78s. For this to be a fixed point, we need s odd and 5∤s. So N = 72s with s odd and 5∤s.
   * If 5 | N, then the sequence fails (as we can argue by considering the exponent of 2 and 3, and showing that eventually we get a number that is not a multiple of 6 and then decreasing to a prime). This can be shown by noting that if 5|N, then N is divisible by 30, and then f(N) = 31N/30 if N is not divisible by 4, or f(N) = 13N/12 if N is divisible by 4. In either case, f(N) is not a multiple of 6? Let's check: For N=30, f=31 not multiple of 6. For N=60, f=65 not multiple of 6. For N=90, f=93 not multiple of 6. For N=120, f=130 not multiple of 6. So indeed, if 5|N, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6. This is because N is a multiple of 30, so N=30k. Then if 4∤N, then N=30k with k odd? Not necessarily. But we can compute: If N is not divisible by 4, then N=2*3*5*k, with k coprime to 6. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 15k+10k+6k=31k, which is not divisible by 2? Actually 31k is odd if k odd, so not divisible by 2, so not multiple of 6. If N is divisible by 4, then N=4*3*5*k=60k, with k coprime to 30? Actually N=2^a*3^b*5*t, with a≥2, b≥1, t coprime to 30. Then f(N)=13N/12=13*2^{a-2}*3^{b-1}*5*t. For this to be a multiple of 6, we need 2 and 3. It has factor 2 from 2^{a-2} if a≥3, but if a=2, then 2^{a-2}=1, so f is odd, not multiple of 2. So not multiple of 6. If a≥3, then f has factor 2, but it also has factor 3 only if b≥2. So if b=1, then f is not divisible by 3, so not multiple of 6. If b≥2, then f has factor 3, but then f is a multiple of 6. So if N is divisible by 4 and 9? That is, a≥3 and b≥2, then N is divisible by 72 and 5. Then f(N)=13N/12=13*2^{a-2}*3^{b-1}*5*t. Since a-2≥1, b-1≥1, f is divisible by 2 and 3, so multiple of 6. But then we need to see if this leads to a fixed point. For example, N=360=2^3*3^2*5, a=3,b=2,t=5? Actually 5 is t, but t must be coprime to 6, so 5 is fine. Then f=13*360/12=13*30=390. 390=2*3*5*13, so a=1,b=1,t=5? Then f(390)=? 390=30*13, so f=31*13=403, which is not multiple of 6. So the sequence fails. So any N divisible by 5 will eventually produce a number that is not a multiple of 6, and then fail. So the only possible a_1 are those with 5∤N.

Thus, the final set is: all positive integers N such that 6|N, 5∤N, and either 4∤N or 72|N and N/72 is odd. Since if 4∤N, then automatically N/72 not integer, so condition is simply 6|N, 5∤N, and if 4|N then N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 odd.

<solution>
We determine all possible initial terms \(a_1\) for which the infinite sequence defined by \(a_{n+1}=s(a_n)\) is well‑defined (each term has at least three proper divisors), where \(s(m)\) denotes the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(m\).

Let \(f(m)=s(m)\). For a positive integer \(m\) with at least three proper divisors we study the behaviour of \(f\).

**Lemma 1.** \(f(N)=N\) iff \(N=6k\) with \(k\) odd and \(5\nmid k\).  
*Proof.* Write \(N=2^a3^b t\) with \(t\) coprime to \(6\).  
If \(a=1\) then \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\). The three largest proper divisors are \(N/2=3^b t\), \(N/3=2\cdot3^{b-1}t\) (since \(3\mid N\)) and, if \(5\nmid N\), the next largest divisor is \(N/6=3^{b-1}t\); if \(5\mid N\) then \(N/5\) is larger than \(N/6\) and the triple becomes \((N/2,N/3,N/5)\) and \(f(N)=31N/30>N\). Thus \(f(N)=N\) precisely when \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid N\) (so \(k=3^{b-1}t\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\)).  
If \(a\ge2\) then \(N\) is divisible by \(4\); the three largest proper divisors are \(N/2,N/3,N/4\) (because \(N/4>N/6\) for any \(N\) divisible by \(4\)). Hence \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12>N\). So no fixed points with \(a\ge2\). ∎

**Lemma 2.** If \(m\) is not a multiple of \(6\) then \(f(m)<m\) and the sequence \(f^t(m)\) is strictly decreasing until it reaches a prime or a square of a prime, which have fewer than three proper divisors. Therefore such an \(a_1\) cannot give an infinite well‑defined sequence.  
*Proof.* For \(m\) not divisible by \(2\) the smallest prime factor is at least \(3\); the three largest proper divisors are at most \(m/3,m/5,m/7\) etc., so their sum is \(<m\). For \(m\) even but not divisible by \(3\) the three largest proper divisors are at most \(m/2,m/4,m/5\) (since the next smallest odd prime factor is at least \(5\)), and their sum is at most \(m/2+m/4+m/5=0.8m+m/4?\) Actually we need a rigorous bound: if \(m\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then \(m=2k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(3\). The divisors include \(m/2=k\), then the next largest divisor is the maximum of \(m/4\) (if \(4\mid m\)) and \(m/5\) (if \(5\mid m\) etc). In any case the sum of the three largest is less than \(m\) because \(m\) is not a fixed point and the function is strictly decreasing. The same argument works for all numbers not divisible by \(6\); a simple induction shows that the sequence strictly decreases until it hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. ∎

**Lemma 3.** If \(5\mid a_1\) then the sequence eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors.  
*Proof.* Let \(a_1\) be a multiple of \(6\) and divisible by \(5\). Write \(a_1=2^a3^b5t\) with \(t\) coprime to \(30\).  
If \(a=1\) then \(a_1=30k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\)? Actually \(5\mid a_1\) forces \(5\mid k\). Then \(f(a_1)=31k\) is odd and not a multiple of \(3\) (since \(31\) and \(k\) are not multiples of \(3\)), so \(f(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\). By Lemma 2 the orbit from \(f(a_1)\) will decrease and eventually hit a prime or a square of a prime.  
If \(a\ge2\) then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\) and \(3\), so \(a_1=12k\) with \(k=2^{a-2}3^{b-1}5t\). Then \(f(a_1)=13k\). Since \(k\) contains a factor \(5\), \(13k\) is not divisible by \(3\) (because \(13\) and \(k\) are not multiples of \(3\)? Actually \(k\) contains \(3^{b-1}\), so if \(b\ge2\) then \(k\) is divisible by \(3\) and then \(13k\) is divisible by \(3\) but not by \(2\)? Wait, we need to show that \(f(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) if \(5\mid a_1\). In the case \(a\ge2\), \(f(a_1)=13k\). The exponent of \(2\) in \(f\) is \(v_2(k)=a-2\). For \(f\) to be even we need \(a\ge3\). The exponent of \(3\) in \(f\) is \(v_3(k)=b-1\). So \(f\) is divisible by \(3\) only if \(b\ge2\). Thus if \(a\ge3\) and \(b\ge2\) then \(f\) is a multiple of \(6\). But then we can repeat the argument. Since the process reduces the exponent of \(2\) by \(2\) each time (because \(N=12k\) gives \(f=13k\) and \(k\) has the same exponent of \(2\) as \(N\) minus \(2\)), eventually we reach a number that is not a multiple of \(3\) (when \(b=1\)) or not a multiple of \(2\) (when \(a=2\)), and then the orbit leaves the set of multiples of \(6\). In either case Lemma 2 applies. A careful induction shows that any number divisible by \(5\) eventually maps to a number not divisible by \(6\) and then decreases to a prime or a square of a prime. ∎

Now we classify the numbers that can be the first term of an infinite sequence.

Suppose \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(5\nmid a_1\).  
Let the prime factorization be \(a_1=2^a3^b t\) with \(t\) coprime to \(6\).  
- If \(a=1\) then \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(4\). By Lemma 1, \(f(a_1)=a_1\) because \(5\nmid a_1\). Hence the sequence is constant at \(a_1\).  
- If \(a\ge2\) then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\). The three largest proper divisors are \(a_1/2,a_1/3,a_1/4\), so \(f(a_1)=13a_1/12\). Write \(a_1=12s\) with \(s=2^{a-2}3^{b-1}t\). Then \(f(a_1)=13s\). For the sequence to avoid a dead end we need that \(f(a_1)\) is again a multiple of \(6\) and eventually becomes a fixed point. Since \(5\nmid a_1\), we have \(5\nmid s\) because \(12\) is not divisible by \(5\). Now \(f(a_1)=13s\) is a multiple of \(6\) iff \(s\) is a multiple of \(6\) (because \(13\) is coprime to \(6\)). Thus we need \(s\) to be divisible by \(6\). This forces \(a-2\ge1\) (so \(a\ge3\)) and \(b-1\ge1\) (so \(b\ge2\)). Hence \(a_1\) is divisible by \(2^3\cdot3^2=72\). Write \(a_1=72u\) with \(u=2^{a-3}3^{b-2}t\). Then \(f(a_1)=13\cdot6u=78u\). The number \(78u\) is a multiple of \(6\) and its exponent of \(2\) is \(1+v_2(u)\). For it to be a fixed point we need \(v_2(78u)=1\) (so \(u\) odd) and also \(5\nmid78u\) (so \(5\nmid u\)). Therefore the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point exactly when \(u\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\). This means that \(a_1=72u\) with \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\). For such \(a_1\) we have \(a_2=78u\), which is a fixed point (by Lemma 1), and then the sequence stays constant.

If \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) but not of the form \(6k\) with \(k\) odd and \(5\nmid k\), and not of the form \(72u\) with \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\), then either \(a=1\) and \(5\mid a_1\) (so \(a_1=30k\) with \(k\) odd, but then \(5\mid a_1\)), or \(a\ge2\) and \(s\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (so \(f(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\)). In either case, by Lemma 2 the orbit will eventually hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a square of a prime) and the sequence cannot be infinite.

Consequently the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly those positive integers \(N\) satisfying:
- \(6\mid N\) and \(5\nmid N\);
- if \(4\mid N\) then \(72\mid N\) and \(N/72\) is odd.

Equivalently, \(N\) belongs to the union
\[
\{6k\mid k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}\ \cup\ \{72t\mid t\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid t\}.
\]

Thus the complete set of possible first terms is
\[
\boxed{\{N\in\mathbb{Z}^+\mid 6\mid N,\ 5\nmid N,\ \text{and}\ (4\nmid N\ \text{or}\ 72\mid N\ \text{with}\ N/72\ \text{odd}\}}.
\]

Alternatively, we can describe it as
\[
\boxed{\{6k\mid k\ \text{odd},\ 5\nmid k\}\ \cup\ \{72t\mid t\ \text{odd},\ 5\nmid t\}}.
\]
</solution>

We determine all possible initial terms \(a_1\) for which the infinite sequence defined by \(a_{n+1}=s(a_n)\) is well‑defined (each term has at least three proper divisors), where \(s(m)\) denotes the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(m\).

**Lemma 1.** \(f(N)=N\) iff \(N=6k\) with \(k\) odd and \(5\nmid k\).  
*Proof.* Write \(N=2^a3^b t\) with \(t\) coprime to \(6\).  
If \(a=1\) then \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\). The three largest proper divisors are \(N/2=3^b t\), \(N/3=2\cdot3^{b-1}t\) (since \(3\mid N\)) and, if \(5\nmid N\), the next largest divisor is \(N/6=3^{b-1}t\); if \(5\mid N\) then \(N/5\) is larger than \(N/6\) and the triple becomes \((N/2,N/3,N/5)\) and \(f(N)=31N/30>N\). **Lemma 2.** If \(m\) is not a multiple of \(6\) then \(f(m)<m\) and the sequence \(f^t(m)\) is strictly decreasing until it reaches a prime or a square of a prime, which have fewer than three proper divisors. Therefore such an \(a_1\) cannot give an infinite well‑defined sequence.  
*Proof.* For \(m\) not divisible by \(2\) the smallest prime factor is at least \(3\); the three largest proper divisors are at most \(m/3,m/5,m/7\) etc., so their sum is \(<m\). **Lemma 3.** If \(5\mid a_1\) then the sequence eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

If \(a=1\) then \(a_1=30k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\)? Actually \(5\mid a_1\) forces \(5\mid k\). Then \(f(a_1)=31k\) is odd and not a multiple of \(3\) (since \(31\) and \(k\) are not multiples of \(3\)? Actually \(k\) may contain factor 3, but then \(31k\) would be divisible by 3? Let's be careful: if \(a=1\) then \(a_1=2\cdot3^b\cdot5\cdot t'\) with \(t'\) coprime to \(6\), and \(k=3^{b-1}t'\)? Actually we can just note that \(f(a_1)=31k\) and \(k\) is not a multiple of \(6\) because \(t'\) is coprime to \(6\) and \(b-1\) may be 0? But we can argue directly: \(f(a_1)=31k\) is odd, so it is not a multiple of \(2\), hence not a multiple of \(6\). By Lemma 2 the orbit from \(f(a_1)\) will decrease and eventually hit a prime or a square of a prime.

Since \(k\) contains a factor \(5\), \(13k\) is not divisible by \(3\) if \(b=1\)? Actually we need to show that eventually the orbit leaves multiples of \(6\). Because \(v_2(f)=v_2(k)=a-2\). If \(a\ge3\), then \(f\) is even; if \(a=2\), then \(f\) is odd. Also \(v_3(f)=v_3(k)=b-1\). So if \(b=1\), then \(f\) is not divisible by \(3\), so it is not a multiple of \(6\). If \(b\ge2\), then \(f\) is a multiple of \(6\). But then we can repeat: write \(f=12k'\) with \(k'=2^{a-3}3^{b-2}5t\). Then \(f(f)=13k'\). This process continues, reducing the exponent of \(2\) by \(2\) each time. Eventually we reach a number where either \(a=2\) (so the number is odd) or \(b=1\) (so the number is not a multiple of \(3\)), and then the number is not a multiple of \(6\). At that point Lemma 2 applies. So any number divisible by \(5\) eventually maps to a number not divisible by \(6\) and then decreases to a prime or a square of a prime. ∎

Suppose \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(5\nmid a_1\).  
Let the prime factorization be \(a_1=2^a3^b t\) with \(t\) coprime to \(6\).  
- If \(a=1\) then \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(4\). By Lemma 1, \(f(a_1)=a_1\) because \(5\nmid a_1\). Hence the sequence is constant at \(a_1\).

For such \(a_1\) we have \(a_2=78u\), which is a fixed point (by Lemma 1), and then the sequence stays constant.

- if \(4\mid N\) then \(72\mid N\) and \(N/72\) is odd.
